My End-to-End Process for Data Science Projects

Why do I write this story? Simply because not every data science project is successful. For sure, you can Google this topic and you will find thousands of reasons. For me, projects that didn't succeed have been the biggest opportunities to improve my process and my skills. In this story, I will briefly summarize my learnings by providing my current (and still improving) process in a data science project. I do not doubt that this process does not apply to everyone simply because there is not a single definition of a "data science project". My aim here is to share my experience by summarizing the different steps of a project. It does not guarantee success, but, at least, this process will try to address all pitfalls that you, as a Data Scientist, can prevent. My role is to create pipelines that consume data and provide insight(s) (see my definition of pipeline in my previous story ). This is the process I am using to do so. Scope the solution The first step is to scope the solution. Sometimes it is very hard to find the balance between expectations and pricing. My recommendation is to drive any decisions by looking at the real need. This would allow putting aside "nice to have" s and prioritize "mandatory features". Start with "why?" (ref: Simon Sinek) To draft the scope of the project, the first step is to work with the client and to understand the pain point and his motivations. In this story, I will use the term client for the person paying for the project. In reality, the client might be a director/VP/CTO of your company. The client is the one that decided to invest in this project. It is mandatory to understand why this person is interested in this project. Maximizing production? Scaling of a process? Avoid failures in his factory? Anything What is the pain point? This is very important for the next step. A good trick is to ask how the client is currently treating his pain point. If there is already a metric capturing the efficiency of the current process. Who will use it? Many times I had the opportunity to draft a scope without even knowing who will use the solution. This is wrong! A project is successful if the solution is used. I have been in projects where the user is not even clearly identified. This is the best way to create something that will never be used. A CEO of a company says: "AI is trendy and cool, we should do something with it". RUN! Let's take an example. A director/VP wants to use AI to optimize stock management in his factory. Great! Then I started talking about the solution but the future user is not even in the room. We start working on the project and we decided that the algorithm will provide an email every morning with a forecast of demand to help the stock manager to make the best decision. I work hard and I succeed in this task. We even develop the infrastructure to support the algorithm. Everything is done and the CEO is happy! Well, guess what? This forecast is not even the manager's biggest concern. He has a hard time figuring out in real-time where and what items are available in the factory. Too bad, a simple interactive dashboard would have done the job. The forecast is useless without this information. The project is a failure. In another situation, the manager needs a forecast every week and not every day, or the forecast needs to be available in realtime not per day All those situations happen. My best recommendation is to bring the future user as early as you can in the conversation because he is the best person to define the needs What? At this point, what I want is a clear success criterion. Client: "I need the algorithm to predict when my assembly line will fail". Me: "Ok", me looking at the operator, "How much in advance do you need this prediction?" (Here, I am trying to define the granularity of the problem.) Operator: "I need the prediction for 2 days in advance, and I need this information in the morning before 7 am". Me: "Great, but you have to understand that chances are the algorithm will produce false alarms and also miss some failures. Are you comfortable with this? What is the current situation? How much cost failure and how much cost asking the maintenance team to work on a machine if it is not needed? How many machines you can check within a day?" I need all those answers to create a success criterion. Me: "How do you solve this problem right now?". Operator: "We have 1000 machines and we have at least 1 of them would fail every day. We cannot check them all, so, we prioritize our investigation by checking the one that has not been checked for a long time". The last information is very important because it tells me where is the bar. If I simulate the current strategy, I can evaluate how much better my solution is. All this information helps me to define a metric with a success criterion. In this type of situation, I rather use a metric like a Hit Rate. The number of true positives within the top N most probable predicted failures. N is the number of interventions the maintenance team can take care of within a day. I rarely communicate metrics like F-Measure or AUC to the client because he cannot use this measurement to decide if the solution is a success or not. At the end of the conversation, we found out that a Hit Rate of 2 would be valuable enough for the solution to be used. Also, thanks to the information I collected, I can price how much money the client is currently wasting and how much money the client can save with a successful solution. Then, I can convert my metric in dollars when I present progress to the client. An important consideration is that my solution will be integrated into an existing system. This system exists for a reason and people are using it. The AI solution will alter this system. This disruption has to be anticipated otherwise the final phase of the project (when people use the solution) may cause unexpected additional costs. This disruption might be so important that the client might finally change his mind and refuse to take the risk of releasing the solution (it happened to me). My recommendation is to introduce this potential disruption in the conversation during the initial scoping of the solution. At the end of the discussion, I ask for a snippet of data, some information about the way we can collect the data, etc I do data analysis (1-2 days) to check if there are no big red flags. Data engineers would also take part in the conversation to draft an architecture. Now, I have the scope, I can move to the next step which is working on the solution. The Proof Of Concept (POC) phase The objective of this phase is to demonstrate that the project is viable and can bring value. To optimize the process in this phase, you have to consider what needs to be constraints. Basically in a POC phase, I cannot constraint the time, the resources, and the accuracy at the same time. Time means the amount of time I can spend on this project. Resources are the number of persons allocated to this project. Accuracy means the accuracy of the model. Theoretically, you can only constraints two of them. In reality, resources are more or less always constrained. Maybe some companies can allocate an "infinite" number of resources on a project. I have never been in such a situation. If you relax accuracy, it corresponds to a "hackathon mode" or "spike". It is useful when you want to investigate an idea, most probably, for a short time. The most realistic choice is to relax time. Some clients have a hard time working in this mode because they have deadlines to respect and they might have the feeling that the POC will never end. But, the trick is to communicate progress to the client in an efficient way. For that reason, I use the Agile methodology. This paradigm has been invented for software development but I adapt it in the context of a data science project. If you are not familiar with the wording, I invite you to read some articles about Agile. Sprint 1: I work generally in a sprint of 2 or 3 weeks depending on the client. The objective of the first sprint is to establish a baseline. A baseline is declared when the first end-to-end pipeline (from data to metric) delivers a number. In this initial sprint, I choose the simplest path. This is the only thing that matters. Get a number as fast as possible. For instance, if some variables have missing values, the simplest is to remove those variables from the dataset. Or if I have to choose a model, the simplest is a linear regression or a logistic regression because there is no parametrization. No creasy preprocessing, only simple steps. Avoid any type of step that would imply fine-tuning or calibration. No PCA, no standardization, nothing. Basically, in this sprint, I set up the CI/CD, the git repository, I write a parser, and I create the simplest version of the pipeline to end up with a number for the metric. The only thing that I need to pay attention to is the evaluation strategy. It is hard to compare performances between 2 pipelines if you switch between 66% train 33% test procedure to a cross-validation procedure. The evaluation strategy needs to be as close as possible from the way the pipeline will be used. In the above example, I would use a growing window for the training set and a sliding window for the test set. Sprint 2: The second sprint is to productize the baseline. With the help of data engineers and DevOps, we set up the architecture to support the pipeline. Some people prefer to wait until the pipeline reaches the level of accuracy as it is defined in the scope. For me, I try to unblock the other teams as early as I can to produce an MVP as soon as possible. For my sprints, I use standard cycles. A stand up meeting every day to check if there are no blockers. We do planning at the beginning of the sprint and demo at the end. We also do retrospective from time to time. At the end of the sprint, we check if the new version of the pipeline provides better results compared to the baseline. If yes, it corresponds to the new baseline for the next sprint. I track this information over sprints and I include it in the demo. I show the plot above to the client during the demo. He can decide 1) to continue for another sprint, 2) to declare that we reach the objectives or 3) to drop the project because the current solution is too far from the expectations and it would take a prohibitive amount of time to finish. This early stoping is beneficial for everyone. For the client, he is not wasting money on a project that might be too expensive and for the team to be reallocated to another project with a higher probability of success. The demo is also the opportunity to validate new assumptions. During each iteration, I increase the complexity of the pipeline. For instance, I gather external data to augment the dataset, I improve the preprocessing, add more features, use a different algorithm, etc The danger here is to over-engineer the pipeline. For instance, I don't try to draft a new deep learning architecture at the second iteration or I don't add crazy complex features to nail one or two outliers that would not affect the metric drastically. To identify what can be the most impactful alteration of the pipeline, I often look at instances that have the largest error to try to find an explanation and derive a new cleaning strategy and/or new features. I order those alterations by the amount of work and start with the simplest. A note about CI/CD and notebook: A measurement of the metric has to be reproducible. So I disregard any data point that has not been produced by the CI/CD pipeline. If the result has been obtained in a Jupyter notebook, I have to convince myself that it can be reproduced on another machine. Notebooks are cool for data analysis but they require strong discipline to make sure the results are reproducible. I know I am not capable of such a thing. For that reason, I only trust numbers from the CI/CD. I have been in a situation where I claimed to the client that we meet the expectation and finally, I was not able to reproduce the results. It is very frustrating and costly for everyone I wish you never have to live in this situation. Productization I strongly recommend you have a look at the article " Hidden Technical Debt in Machine Learning Systems ". The machine learning code is a small piece of the entire solution. It is important to have a clear picture of the additional steps needed for productization. productization requires the support of data engineers and DevOps. Hopefully, the POC is done and we met the success criterion. Good!! Now we need to productize the pipeline. There are two main ways to proceed: Hands-off . The results of the POC are translated into specifications. A team of software developers take over, rewrite the entire code, and productize the pipeline. Push to prod . My code is reused "as is" and with the help of data engineers and DevOps, we build an infrastructure around it. Both approaches have pros and cons. The hands-off approach will make sure that the solution is more stable. Because a data scientist is not always an experimented developer (no offense here), productization of the code is often in the hand of other people. It is often the case in a large company. A detail, in this situation, the ownership of the code does not belong to the data scientist anymore. So fixing bugs is done by the software development team. The second approach is to ask the data scientist to wrap his code into a library and it will be used in another system. For instance, in a lambda function in the cloud. This approach is faster since we avoid rewriting code. The code is "cleaned" and the debts are paid. If you follow my recommendations in my previous story , this step can be done efficiently. Finally, the owner of the code remains the data scientist and I am in charge of fixing bugs. This way is often chosen in small companies and startups where a limited number of people can work on each project. I prefer to use the second option. I will explain my reason with more detail in the next section. Briefly, when using the first approach, if I want to release improvements of the solution or to add some features, I would have to work on my code base, rewrite specifications, wait for the developer team to update the solution, etc This can take months. A fast cycle would imply to minimize hands-off. Maintaining and improving the pipeline Ok, now my pipeline is in production. However, it is important to create a process to capture the feedback of the operator to continuously improve the solution. For instance, The operator is using the product daily but he proposed some improvements for the algorithm. For instance, instead of predicting if a failure would occur, he prefers to have a score or a probability of failure. To derive a score from the prediction, it would require a minor change in the pipeline. Just returning the probability instead of the predicted class. What I try to do is to set up a feedback process as soon as the first version of the pipeline is accessible (sprint 2-3). This feedback loop would also help me to adjust over the sprints but when the product is released, this continuous improvement remains. To have a good interaction with the user, I want improvement to be released as fast as possible. For that reason, I avoid the hands-off strategy to do the improvement myself and get feedback faster. The feedback loop is also important to capture the decisions made by the operator. When decisions don't match the prediction of the pipelines, it means we can improve the pipeline by incorporating the "reasons" the operator took his decision. Over time, the role of the operator will change and get simpler. One consequence is the ability to scale his capabilities. Ultimately, a predictive pipeline can become a prescriptive pipeline. Then, the prediction is not interpreted by the operator anymore and directly connected to take action. This introduces my last point: Company reorganization It is important to understand that introducing an AI solution in a company, will disrupt the usual way the employee would work. Sometimes, the employees' role changes and additional skills might be needed. For example, if you introduce a chatbot to help the customer support team, this team might be reduced or reallocated to other tasks. The solution might also cause some business reorientation in the client company. I have seen some companies changing their strategy from selling products to selling services because of the new solution. This is often not anticipated by the client. Sometimes, it can cause the project to stop for a while. It happened to me that the company would have been so much affected by the algorithm that the reorganization and the integration of the solution in their system would require much more investment. They finally decided not to use it. I try to introduce this subject very early in the process to make sure the client would anticipate the situation. Please feel free to connect. My LinkedIn can be found here Denne blog er oprindeligt bragt p Medium

Vi bliver ndt til at tale om selvmordet. Og om at man ikke fr 12 hver dag. S vi kan leve med, at nogle gange har man det skidt

Siden jeg i efterret 2018 s Tuva Novotnys film ' Blindzone', har jeg gerne villet tale med instruktren om den. Fordi Novotny med ' Blindzone' har skabt en film, der viser, hvad der kan ske, nr man ikke fr delt sine mrke tanker med andre, eller hvis forldre, familie, venner og i det hele taget omverdenen undlader at reagere, nr man ser tegn p, at et menneske har det psykisk skidt.' Blindzone', der var nomineret til Nordisk Rds Filmpris i 2019 og nu er nomineret til en European Film Academy Award, er som et hb imod den isolation, og at den virkede s strkt p mig, hnger sammen med, at den er indspillet i t take. Det er med til at gre handlingen realistisk, nrvrende og intens, og jeg kunne ikke se den uden bagefter at tale med nogen om den.Da jeg s Jonas Alexander Arnbys film ' Selvmordsturisten', kom nogle af de samme tanker, men p en helt anden mde. Den bde visuelt og dramaturgisk eksperimenterende ' Selvmordsturisten' har Nikolaj Coster-Waldau i den brende hovedrolle. Han spiller forsikringsagenten Max, der er terminalt syg af krft i hjernen og s bange for, hvordan sygdommen vil ndre ham, at han tjekker ind p et mystisk udenlandsk luksushotel med speciale i selvmord for at f en vrdig dd. Ogs efter at have set ' Selvmordsturisten' havde jeg brug for at tale med nogen om, hvad et liv er vrd - p et mere eksistentielt plan.Ud over at vre instruktr er Tuva Novotny manuskriptforfatter og skuespiller, kendt fra blandt andet ' Krigen' og ' Borg'. I dag siger hun sjldent ja til at medvirke som skuespiller, men i Jonas Arnbys ' Selvmordsturisten' har hun alligevel en stor rolle som Max' hustru, Lrke.I anledning af premieren sidste uge p ' Selvmordsturisten' er Tuva Novotny og Jonas Arnby mdtes for at tale om at skabe film om mental usundhed.IJONAS ARNBY: Jeg kendte dig kun som skuespiller, da jeg tog kontakt til dig. ' Blindzone' var ikke kommet ud endnu, tror jeg. TUVA NOVOTNY: Jeg havde ikke lavet den. ARNBY: Jeg havde lagt mrke til, at du som skuespiller har en naturlighed og en utrolig velbalanceret og trovrdig tilstedevrelse i scenerne. Jeg havde hrt rygter om, at du ikke ville spille s meget mere, men spurgte alligevel. NOVOTNY: Jeg har ubetinget respekt for kunstnere, der springer ud i ting, som mske er usikre kort. Og det gr du jo. Du er en af de f, som tr gre det. Hvis man kigger p Lars von Triers film, er det ikke altid, jeg forstr dem eller finder dem fantastiske, men jeg vil altid bakke op om nogen, der tr kaste sig ud i noget nyskabende og modigt, ligesom Trier gr, og ligesom jeg faktisk ogs synes, at du gr. Da jeg lste manuskriptet til ' Selvmordsturisten', havde jeg det umiddelbart sdan, at jeg gerne ville arbejde sammen med dig. Derfor sagde jeg ja til at spille igen. ARNBY: Da jeg s hrte, at du skulle lave ' Blindzone', viste det sig, at vi havde flles interesse for den tematik. NOVOTNY: Men vores indgang er fuldstndig forskellig. Dit univers er meget strre. ARNBY: Der er en helt anden realisme i din film, og i forhold til emnet selvmord har den en anden nerve. Hvor det emne i min film mere bliver brugt som et dramaturgisk greb til at skabe pres p en hovedkarakter, der skal tage nogle beslutninger omkring sit liv. Min film er i mindre grad en politisk film om selvmord, tror jeg. NOVOTNY: Ja, min har helt klart en agenda, der henvender sig til sundhedsvsen, skoler og andre institutioner. Med min ville jeg rejse rundt for at informere om prventiv suicidalmetodik.Jeg synes ogs, at din film er politisk, men p et filosofisk plan. ARNBY: Jeg kan godt lide at dyrke, at man str lidt uden for realismen. ' Selvmordsturisten' har ikke den virkelighed, som ' Blindzone' har.Med ' Selvmordsturisten' skal man vre indstillet p, at man forhbentlig vil se tingene i et lidt andet perspektiv og kan relatere til den konstruerede realisme i filmen. Min forrige film, ' Nr dyrene drmmer', var jo ogs sdan et mikrokosmos. Der var ingen tid og intet sted. Det var en konceptuel arena for noget, vi kan genkende. Jeg placerer mig lidt ved siden af for at f et andet kig ind i det.IIEr selvmord et emne, der betyder noget for alle mennesker? NOVOTNY: Da jeg begyndte at arbejde med tematikken, gik det op for mig, at selvmord er s betndt et emne, at selv om man ikke har oplevet det tt p, s er det noget, der optager mennesker ret meget. Fordi det stadig er s uforsteligt og tabubelagt og omgivet af skam. Jeg tror klart, det er noget, der har betydning for mange. For mit vedkommende handler det om at lfte det op til overfladen og diskutere, hvordan vi snakker om selvmordstematik.Tidligere, historisk og religist, har man haft en id om, at man ikke skal snakke om det, blandt andet fordi det har en smitteeffekt. Senere rs suicidalforskning viser, at hvis vi skal arbejde prventivt, s findes der retoriske metoder at omtale suicidaltematik p, s det netop reducerer smitteeffekt, afstigmatiserer og bryder isolation omkring psykisk sygdom og selvmord. For mig handlede det meget om netop at bne og sige: Ja, det er noget, der optager mennesker rigtig meget, hvad enten man lser om det i avisen eller har personlig erfaring med det. En del af den prventive retorik er at tale om ensomhed og isolation og dele med andre, s man ikke er alene, og det synes jeg, at begge vores film viser. Vi skal bryde den isolation, som kendetegner mange med de her tanker. Hvad siger du, Jonas?. ARNBY: Der er ikke s meget at tilfje, tnker jeg. NOVOTNY: Men hvor jeg er interesseret i suicidalkommunikation, har du et helt andet interessefelt. ARNBY: Overvejelserne om selvmord i min film er en mde, hvorp Max kan f kontrol over sin exit. Han er bange for at miste sig selv og for ikke at vre den, han har vret, i sin relation til sin kone Lrke. Den eneste mde, han kan kontrollere sin skbne p, er ved selvmordet. Ikke at jeg gr omkring med den store objektive verdensanalyse, men i det ligger der noget om os mennesker i de moderne vestlige samfund. Vi prver at styre alting, og vi vil ogs bestemme, hvornr vi skal herfra.Mske er Max en essens af det. Vi er s bange for det uventede og det tilfldige og vil s gerne vide, hvad der sker med os. NOVOTNY: Med ' Selvmordsturisten' gik det efterhnden op for mig, at den overhovedet ikke handler om selvmord. Den handler om ddshjlp og om, hvorvidt vi som mennesker selv skal have muligheden for at beslutte, hvordan vi skal afslutte vores liv. ARNBY: Jamen den tematik har jeg ikke lyst til at g ind i. Jeg er lidt bange for, at den vil overskygge det, filmen i virkeligheden handler om for mig: om at finde meningen med livet.Jeg hber, at folk vil se den her film, fordi de gerne vil opleve en film om relationer, om livet, om krligheden. Aktiv ddshjlp er kun et element i filmen, og det bliver ikke skildret, som det i virkeligheden foregr. NOVOTNY: I know. Rent filosofisk og eksistentielt handler ' Selvmordsturisten' om, at vi gerne vil have et overskueligt liv, vi kan kontrollere helt til og med vores sidste frden. Og om, hvad det vigtigste i livet i virkeligheden er.ARNBY: Da det gr op for Max, hvad der kommer til at ske med ham, er relationen til Lrke det vigtigste for ham. NOVOTNY: P grund af mental sygdom hnder det, at nogle mennesker, som mske er isolerede fra deres relationer, tror, at de vil gre andre en tjeneste ved at tage deres eget liv. I virkeligheden er det jo fuldstndig stik modsat.Ethvert selvmord delgger s meget for mennesker omkring vedkommende. S selvflgelig betyder relationen til andre meget, og selvflgelig er ens eksistens vigtig for andre.Ogs selv om man ikke tror det selv i en situation, hvor man har det rigtig skidt eller ligesom Max har en ddsdom hngende over sig. Jeg tror, at det er en af de vigtige ting at tage med sig ud af biografen, nr man har set ' Selvmordsturisten'. ARNBY: Og ' Blindzone'. NOVOTNY: Ja. Og det er vigtigt at kommunikere ikke mindst til unge. At det ikke er en vej ud. Det er det bare ikke.IIIARNBY: Skabte din film debat inden for ungdomspsykiatrien i Norge?. NOVOTNY: Den blev virkelig godt modtaget og bliver nu brugt i undervisningssituationer i Rde Kors og andre organisationer. Til brn fra 15 r og opefter. Aldersgrnsen er s hj, fordi vi aner, hvordan pigen prvede at tage sit liv, og det at omtale metode kan have en smitteeffekt. Jeg ville nske, vi kunne have styret det, s vi ikke s det, men det kunne ikke lade s gre, fordi vi optog i one take. Da jeg rejste rundt i Norge med visninger af den og samtaler med psykologer og s videre om den, var modtagelsen positiv med en undtagelse.Der var en visning, hvor et menneske stillede sig op og sagde, at man ikke br lave film om selvmord, og at man i det hele taget ikke br diskutere tematikken. ARNBY: Det er interessant, at du siger, at emnet er tabuiseret. Mens jeg arbejdede med ' Selvmordsturisten', talte jeg jo om filmen derhjemme, og jeg skulle lige finde ud af, hvordan jeg talte med mine brn om, at jeg var i gang med en film med den titel. NOVOTNY: Mange mangler verbale vrktjer til at tale om selvmord p en god mde. Jeg mener, at vi skal kunne tale om det. Jeg tror, faren ligger i at normalisere begrebet selvmord.Det skal ikke vre normalt. Men man kan normalisere at tale om, at folk har det skidt, at man kan vre et meget ensomt sted, og at man skal huske at dele med hinanden, s man ikke kommer dertil, for selvmord er ikke et alternativ.Alts sdan helt basale mder at kommunikere om mental sundhed og usundhed p, s man kommer vk fra selvmordet og i stedet diskuterer det med at have det drligt for netop at reducere risikoen for at komme s langt ud. ' Blindzone' og efter min mening egentlig ogs ' Selvmordsturisten' nsker at gre op med, at vi alle sammen skal vre perfekte, hjtprsterende 12-talsfolk hele vejen igennem for at klare os i livet.Livet bliver aldrig perfekt. Man fr ikke 12 hver dag, og vi skal finde nogle vrktjer, s vi kan leve med, at nogle gange har man det skidt.Man kan endda blive alvorligt syg, og hen ad vejen kommer man til at miste nogle mennesker.Det m vi lre at leve med og huske p, at livet bestr af faser, og at det som regel bliver bedre.Har man ret til at tage sit eget liv? ARNBY: Jeg ved ikke prcis, hvad jeg skal svare.Der er mange former for selvmord, og vi skal alle sammen igennem eksistentielle udfordringer.Jeg vil ikke snakke om det ud fra et generelt perspektiv, men for mit vedkommende har jeg det sdan, at hvis jeg var terminalt syg og havde mulighed for at vlge en smertefri dd, ville jeg gre det. Det ville da vre en befrielse, hvis man var i et stort smertehelvede, og nogen kunne hjlpe en med at gre en ende p det. P den anden side - det er jo en hypotetisk tanke, s lnge jeg sidder her og ikke er syg. Jeg tnker bare..hvis jeg var ddssyg, og der ingen chance var for helbredelse, og jeg vidste, at jeg ville ndre personlighed og f en smertefuld exit, ville jeg til enhver tid hellere.... NOVOTNY: Men s sker der jo ogs mirakler.Og der m vre plads i systemet til en individuel vurdering, tnker jeg. Jeg snakkede med nogle, hvor et familiemedlem har ligget i 20 r uden at vre kontaktbar. Og der er systemet rigidt, for man fr ikke lov til at slukke. ARNBY: Det er enormt svrt at generalisere om. NOVOTNY: Men med hensyn til abortsprgsmlet mener jeg grundlggende, at vi skal have lov til at bestemme over vores kroppe. Der er en kmpelobbyisme imod abort, hvor man blandt andet argumenterer med, at vi ikke skal lege Gud. Men vi leger jo Gud hver dag med livsforlngende medicin og lignende.IVNOVOTNY: For ' Blindzone' havde vi en kommunikationsplan, hvor vi i begyndelsen slet ikke talte om selvmord. Vi talte om mental usundhed, fordi ' Blindzone' i bund og grund handler om prventiv kommunikation og alt andet omkring selvmord. Det samme gr ' Selvmordsturisten'. Begge film viser, hvor vigtigt det er at tale med hinanden.ARNBY: Uanset om man har en sygdom eller et depressivt sind, kan det vre svrt at tale med en anden. Man har nogle forestillinger om sig selv og om den anden i en relation. I ' Selvmordsturisten' kan Max ikke vre den, han tror, at Lrke nsker, han skal vre. For at kunne tale rigtigt sammen bliver man ndt til at smide de forestillinger, man har om hinanden. NOVOTNY: Det er jo en samfundsmssig sygdom, at vi har en forkert forestilling om, hvad det vil sige at vre strk. Pia Tjelta, der har hovedrollen som mor i min film og er med i alle mine film og ogs er min veninde, skulle lave et interview for et norsk blad, men endte med i stedet at skrive en klumme om at vre en strk kvinde i dag. Hun konkluderer, at det at vre strk er at turde vre srbar og svag.Hvis man bner og tr at vre en hel person med srbarheder og svagheder, bliver man strk. Desvrre konfronteres man hele tiden med at skulle leve op til den der historiske, forldede id om at vre strk.Men nr man sidder som Max over for Lrke eller som mor over for et depressivt barn, kan det alts vre virkelig svrt at n ind til at tale om, at det er strkt at vre svag. Hvad skal man sige? At man ogs selv har det svrt, og at man forstr? NOVOTNY: Jeg lner mig rigtig meget op ad syv rs psykoterapi, og da handler det ikke s meget om at sige, at jeg ogs har det svrt. I stedet kan jeg sige, at jeg heller ikke altid ved, hvordan jeg skal hndtere ting, men at det bliver bedre, og at man ikke er alene. Det kan bne. ARNBY: Der er en scene, jeg har tnkt p i forbindelse med ' Selvmordsturisten'. Som ikke er i filmen, og som jeg indimellem ville nske var der. Hvor Lrke tager Max med ud til badebroen og skubber ham i vandet for at f hul p den boble, han er i. Det behver nemlig ikke vre samtale eller rundbordsterapi. Nogle gange har man brug for noget andet. For at f vist, at man er blevet forstet. For at nogen bryder ind i ens isolation. NOVOTNY: I filmen ville sdan en scene have vret for overtydelig, synes jeg. Men det, vi taler om nu, er et plan dybere end det, vi snakkede om lige fr. Nu snakker vi i virkeligheden om det, alle mennesker dybest set er bange for: at vre fuldstndig alene. Og uanset om et menneske har brug for samtale eller fysisk handling, drejer det sig om at vise, at man er der og gerne vil vre sammen med det menneske. ARNBY: Og nogle gange hjlper handling p en anden mde end snak. P et tidspunkt havde jeg en psykolog, som foreslog mig at tage ud til stranden og bade, hvis jeg havde brug for at se tingene lidt anderledes. Det bruger jeg stadig. Jeg siger ikke, at man er helet bagefter, men der er en eller anden form for nulstilling i at lgge kroppen i vand. Man gr noget.Man bryder et mnster. Hvilket ellers tit kan det svreste. Ligesom hvis man har det skidt, og nogen siger: S lb dog en tur. Og man tnker, at hvordan i alverden skulle man kunne det, nr man ikke synes, man kan bevge benene? Og hvor skulle man i vrigt lbe hen?. NOVOTNY: Med sm brn, der skriger, kan man lige puste dem blidt i ansigtet, s de bliver afbrudt. S hnder det, at de stopper op og finder ud af, at de ikke behver at skrige.Det er lidt det samme. Man bryder et mnster.VHar I set andre film, der er gode til at diskutere de ting, vi har talt om? NOVOTNY: Min personlige motivation med alle projekter, jeg laver, er at give trst. At forsge at give metoder eller tanker til at overkomme den frygt, der kan virke kulsort. Og for mig er der nogle instruktrer, som eksistentielt giver mig en flelse af trst. Jeg synes, at Spike Jonze er fantastisk p den mde. Lige s syrede og fantastiske universer, han skaber, lige s dybt menneskeligt og eksistentielt trstende synes jeg, de er. Om det s er i ' Her' eller i ' Where the Wild Things Are'. De film handler om noget vidt forskelligt, men ogs om ensomhed og om, at det nok skal g. ARNBY: Jeg har set meget p ' Jacob's Ladder', der handler om en Vietnam-veteran, der srger over sit dde barn. Egentlig er den ret banal, men den har fascineret mig med sin strke, subjektive hovedrolle. Den er meget billedlig, men i modstning til ' Selvmordsturisten' har den ikke meget undertekst. NOVOTNY: Jeg var vild med ' Jacob's Ladder', da jeg var yngre. Nr jeg ser din film, tnker jeg p noget helt andet. Jeg tnker cinematiske, eksistentielle, konceptuerede universer, som jeg for eksempel ogs har set det i Joachim Triers ' Thelma'. Den handler ogs om supersvre ting, men ophjer det til et andet univers. Jeg synes, at I har et slgtskab. ARNBY: Noget af det, jeg stter allerstrst pris p, er at prve at holde dialogen minimal i forhold til essensen af scenen. Det vigtige ligger faktisk imellem det, der bliver sagt. Og filmformatet er trods alt stadig, heldigvis, et format, hvor man har mulighed for at skabe den ndvendige luft, s publikum kan drage deres egen konklusion. Det er der sjldent plads til i tv, selv om det mere og mere bliver sdan, at man prver at lave noget, der ikke er skret over samme skabelon. Har du nogensinde vret fuldt fokuseret p en tv-serie?. NOVOTNY: Det har jeg. For tiden elsker jeg en, der hedder ' Easy' p Netflix. Den er socialrealistisk, men meget humoristisk. Lidt Woody Allensk. ARNBY: Jeg ser alt. Godt og drligt. Men jeg kan ikke se tv-serier, uden at jeg ogs er i gang med mobilen eller et eller andet. NOVOTNY: Det er vel ogs noget med koncentrationen.Nr du ser film, ved du, at du kun skal sidde halvanden time cirka. Nr du streamer tv-serier, ved du, at det tager.... ARNBY: 18 timer. Som for eksempel ' Roma' (mexicansk film, red.) gr: Den benytter sig af en meget gammel fortlleteknik og rytme, hvor man brugte lange takes, fordi det var det, man havde rd til. Da sidder man jo klistret, fordi det er s usdvanligt nu at se et langt take i en scene. ARNBY: Jeg havde det ikke helt sdan med ' Roma'. P mange planer har det vret s spndende for mig at mde dig.dorte.hygum@pol.dk P grund af mental sygdom hnder det, at nogle mennesker, som mske er isolerede fra deres relationer, tror, at de vil gre andre en tjeneste ved at tage deres eget liv. Five elements of an AI business case

There are five critical elements the business executive needs to include in a business case for an AI solution, and AI implementations fail if the responsible business executive does not detail these five areas.More and more business executives are facing their first AI business case. Executives such as CHROs (Chief Human Resource Officers), CFOs, CMOs, or CSCOs (Chief Supply Chain Officers).They need to create an AI business case with limited experience, if any, within their organization. The IT organization will likely handle the business case's complex technical aspects, whereas the business executive is left with detailing the AI solution's business impact.There are five critical elements the business executive needs to include in a business case for an AI solution. They are 1) the business problem solved by AI, 2) the details of the AI solution, 3) Scalability & maintenance, 4) AI Financials, and 5) the stakeholders. AI implementations fail if the responsible business executive does not detail these five areas.The classical business caseHowever, before looking at these five elements, it is essential to understand that an AI project's business case will deviate from classical business cases in four distinct areas. These four areas are:First, AI is a ''discovery-driven'' investment, resulting in a business case with no clearly defined KPIs. The reason is that there is a limited history of use cases, knowledge, and data about AI implementations in most organizations. As a result, business executives need to address two challenges. First, the executive responsible for the AI project and the CFO or finance organization must understand that this is not a traditional business case. The risk profile of an AI business case is different. If the AI project risks are not made explicit, the project may get defunded. Secondly, the business case's key executives will need to determine the business case metrics and track them. These KPIs will likely be less standard KPIs or less robust in the initial business case.Second, AI projects are cross-functional projects. They often involve more business functions in addition to the IT function than the average IT project. Therefore, project and business case governance is more complex, the budget impact is broader, and the need for collaboration higher. When creating an AI business case, ensure that the project's governance is detailed, the effect on budgets across the organization is agreed upon, and the implementation plan includes dealing with the need for communication across the organization to enable frictionless collaboration.Third, ensure that the data environment is ready for implementation. AI projects far too often stall or even fail because the data is not available or not usable as-is. Information is collected for a purpose and in a format that supports a specific analysis. As a result, the data may not support new forms of analysis intended for the AI solution proposed. Every AI business case needs to highlight the importance of pre-existing quality and relevant data or include a budget to ensure that such a dataset is created as part of the business case.Fourth and last, the AI business case needs to include provisions for the appropriate skills and leadership knowledge to execute the project and subsequently manage the environment when in production. This may result in the need to hire more data science or developer expertise and include costs for developing organizational and leadership competencies.Once an organization has internalized these four differences in AI business cases, they can work on the details required in the five elements of an AI business case.Five elements of an AI business caseThere is extensive coverage of AI business case development from a technical perspective, including the challenges around the data environment, what a business case needs to consider concerning the overall system architecture, and critical security, risk, and governance. But there is only limited coverage of the business elements for the AI business case. Below are five factors to consider as part of every business case for an AI implementation.The business problem: The first but obvious question is what business problem or opportunity are you solving? While this seems a trivial question, far too often, the actual business problem is not clearly articulated. As a consequence, it becomes harder to measure the impact or result of the project. This statement applies to any project, but because most organizations are still learning about AI's use and effects, it is even more critical for AI projects. A clear articulation of the business problem to be solved and how to measure the project's impact is the first step to get approval for an AI project, especially if the finance organization is not experienced in ''discovery-driven'' investments.The solution: Spending time on detailing the actual AI solution is the second area of focus. This is critical for several reasons. These include the fact that most projects are new to the organization, so what the solution is, how it will work, at scale, and who will be involved in using the solution will be new to the organization. Ensure that the solution fits as part of the organization's overall strategy and is linked to the key leaders'objectives and their organizations implementing the solution.Scalability & Maintenance: Most AI projects are initiated as a smaller POC project with the intent of scaling the solution rapidly. The business case must outline how to scale the solution, technically, business-wise, and on the required skills. Lastly, the necessary training and development needed within the organization implementing the solution. Furthermore, maintenance of the full-scale solution is more complicated with AI projects since the algorithms evolve as well the regulatory and legal environments around AI. Lastly, be realistic with the timelines for the scalability of the AI project. An initial review of projects over the last three years shows that most organizations are overly optimistic about implementation and scalability timelines.Financials: The AI business will need to be approved by the finance organization, as is the case for any project. Given that most AI business cases lack well-proven and standard data and metrics used in traditional business cases, the finance organization will often challenge the business case because it is a ''discovery-driven'' investment. Consider whether simulating data where non exists could be a possible option. Approaching the AI business case as a venture capitalist is a wise approach, thus assuming less qualified KPIs, a clear, quantifiable identification of risks, and an iterative approach to proving the business value as you scale the solution. Lastly, the business case's financial part should also define possible failure criteria, so these are clear from the outset.Stakeholders: AI projects involve more stakeholders than traditional IT projects. In addition to several entities within the IT organization, there will be several business stakeholders. The critical stakeholder will be the organization implementing the solution, but the HR organization and the legal organization will likely need to be involved. Thus, ensuring a broader stakeholder communication plan and continuous reporting is a critical part of the AI business case.There will be other factors to include in an AI business case, but the five above are the most critical to have in the business case any organization builds. Also, understand that all business case documents should be treated as live documents, especially AI business cases.CEOs and senior business executives need to appreciate that AI business cases will differ from traditional business cases in several ways. This is one of several critical areas that need to be addressed if AI implementations are to scale across all organizations' sizes.

Don't democratize data science

A plethora of online courses and tools promise to democratize the field, but just learning a few basic skills does not a true data scientist make. Every few years, some academic and professional field gets a lot of cachet in the popular imagination. Right now, that field is data science. As a result, a lot of people are looking to get into it. Add to that the news outlets calling data science sexy and various academic institutes promising to make a data scientist out of you in just a few months, and you've got the perfect recipe for disaster. Of course, as a data scientist myself, I don't think the problem lies in people choosing data science as a profession. If you're interested in working with data, understanding business problems, grappling with math, and you love coding, you're probably going to thrive in data science. You'll get a lot of opportunities to use math and coding to develop innovative solutions to problems and will likely find the work rewarding. The main issue here is that the motivations people have for entering the field are often flawed. For some, the appeal is money, while others like the way the title sounds. Even worse, some people are probably just responding to the herd mentality that our society has instilled. For instance, not long ago, every graduate aspired to do an MBA. And I myself am guilty of the same. It took me a GMAT test and a couple of rejections to realize that I didn't really want the degree. Ultimately, those rejections were the best thing that happened to me because, after that, I finally looked at data science as an option. Once I got into it, I found that I love the math involved and all the different ways in which I get to use data science to help solve problems for businesses. Today, I see that data science has somehow acquired the same stature that the MBA once had. A lot of people want to do it, but they don't know what the job really entails. And this has resulted in a lot of people calling themselves data scientists and a lot of bad decision making. In fact, a lot of people considering entering the profession probably don't even know what data science is. Today, the whole field has been democratized by the availability of so much material. A plethora of MOOCs from the best instructors cover concepts from basic to advanced, and you can easily find packages that let you create models with just a few lines of code. I genuinely love the fact that there are so many resources to learn and practice data science. But this democratization has created a few problems of its own. In this piece, I want to briefly look at some of these problems and the adverse effect they could have on the field. Automated data science? A lot of AutoML packages aim at democratizing data science. They provide a repository of models, automate the hyperparameter tuning process, and sometimes offer a way to put these models into production. The availability of such packages has led a lot of people to think that data science could be fully automated, eliminating the need for data scientists altogether. Or, if the processes can't be automated, these tools will allow anyone to become a data scientist. I sincerely disagree. I have found such codebases useful at times, but they look at data science purely from a coding perspective. In my view, data science involves a lot of work apart from modeling. The work of data science includes understanding and identifying the problem at hand and setting up the right evaluation metrics. You also have to analyze the profitability of the project: most businesses don't want to spend money on projects that don't positively affect the bottom line. You can work with existing data, but sometimes you might need to come up with ways to set up new data pipelines to gather data to solve the problem. This requires talking to the stakeholders and gaining a holistic understanding of the problem. A data scientist also needs to be able to carry out data munging and feature creation to churn more performance out of existing models. In the end, model testing and setting the feedback loop require endless hours of discussions with the business and is pretty specific to each project. Someone who just runs code might not be able to add value to such discussions as they don't really understand what goes behind the models they have used in AutoML. Then there comes the issue of domain knowledge. Processes that are acceptable in a retail domain are not applicable in the finance domain where a small change could result in your customers losing a lot of money. Some things just can't be automated since they require domain knowledge and an understanding of the business you're working with. More importantly, an automated pipeline can't be held responsible if a project doesn't work or if your model fails in production. A good data scientist will try to figure out ways to sort out production issues as they arise as well as creating a machine learning pipeline specific to the project to mitigate such issues. The code-runner mentality I have become skeptical of what I call the New Data Scientist. Almost every day, I seem to meet a person calling themselves a data scientist when they are just glorified code runners, which refers to a person who just runs the code without understanding what goes on behind it. With so many academies and institutes providing boot-camp-based courses, code runners are in abundance right now. I get a lot of requests where someone asks me whether they should take a certified course from XYZ institute or a boot camp from ABC academy. My answer is neither. I find that these institutes that promise to make data scientists in droves are mainly just in the money-making business. Ultimately, going through a few notebooks and running somebody else's code doesn't truly make a person a data scientist. Now, don't get me wrong. If someone learns best through a top-down approach where they run some code first and then read in depth about the principles behind it, that's perfectly fine. Data science is about more than just running code, though. Until you really understand the math and theory behind all the code, you haven't mastered data science. The Dunning-Kruger effect The Dunning-Kruger effect is a type of cognitive bias in which a person with a little bit of knowledge about some subject overestimates their abilities because they're unaware of how little they actually know. I see this in action constantly in data science. In fact, it might be more pronounced in this field than any other! I tend to think of this as a novice effect. It's a problem that plagues people in the early stages of learning a new skill. In my view, there are three stages to a data scientist's journey. The Dunning-Kruger Stage - You created your first model and think you know everything there is to know about data science. The "I Don't Know Anything" Stage - You go to a conference or talk to your peers and suddenly realize that there is so much more to learn. The "Lifelong Learning" Stage - You give in to the fact that there are always going to be some things you won't know that just got introduced and so there is lifelong learning involved in pursuing data science. Now, the Dunning-Kruger effect is something that most of the beginners will face. The joy of running your first program and executing it perfectly really takes you to the top of the world. And it's totally fine to be at this stage. The problem comes when newcomers are incapable of leaving this stage and moving on to the next ones in a timely fashion. I have seen a few people who get stuck at this stage because they got into data science with the wrong expectations, thinking that it's sexy and exciting, without understanding the field's depth. These types of people tend to think they can just use existing models to solve problems and that they get by without understanding the math. For instance, I recently interviewed a guy who had two years of experience in the field. He seemed confident. He had used data science in his job and had worked on a couple of Kaggle projects. The first few minutes of the interview went really well. He explained the higher-level concepts well enough that I decided to dig a little deeper into his mathematical understanding of the techniques he had applied in his projects. And that was where things changed. I asked him to tell me about the log loss function. When he said, " But we have packages for doing all this " I realized that this guy had never left the first stage. The availability of ready-made packages and superficial courses is democratizing the field of data science. But there is just so much more to the job that comes from hands-on experience, communicating with people, and being able to listen to different perspectives. So, while some people may think of data science as a pure coding job, it's not just about becoming a coding superstar. It's about finding the right problems that are useful for the business and coming up with best ways to solve them. To do that, you need domain knowledge, humility, a little bit of math, and, most importantly, a lifelong desire to learn. Denne blog er oprindeligt bragt p builtin.com
Pro.ing.dk/datatech    2020-05-26    e7b90384    #3

Det er kun t af flere dilemmaer, som kresteparret opdager, efterhnden som de fr et dagligt forhold til Adam.McEwan ridser det op p denne mde:.UDDRAG Fra ' Maskiner som mig'Fra kapitel fire: Miranda gik hen til komfuret og lavede kaffe. Mens hun stod med ryggen til, sagde hun muntert: Charlie. Din opfrsel er helt til grin. Er den?. Fjendtlig. Og hvad s?. Hun kom hen til bordet med to krus og en kande mlk. Hendes bevgelser var hurtige og afslappede.Havde jeg ikke vret der, havde hun mske givet sig til at synge. Hendes hnder lugtede svagt af citron. Jeg troede, hun ville lgge en hnd p min skulder, og blev straks spndt, men hun gik hen i den anden ende af kkkenet igen. Lidt efter sagde hun med en vis forsigtighed: Du kunne hre os i gr aftes. Jeg kunne hre dig. Og du er vred. Jeg svarede ikke.Det behver du ikke at vre. Jeg trak p skulderen. Hun sagde: Hvis jeg var get i seng med en vibrator, ville du s have haft den samme flelse?. Han er ikke en vibrator. Hun kom hen med kaffen og satte sig ved siden af mig. Hun udviste venlighed og bekymring og gav mig rollen som det surmulende barn og forsgte at f mig til at glemme, at hun var ti r yngre.Det, der foregik imellem os, var vores hidtil mest intime ordveksling.Fjendtlig? Hun havde aldrig omtalt mine sindstilstande med s meget som et ord. Hun sagde: Han har nogenlunde den samme bevidsthed. Vibratorer har ikke meninger.De luger ikke staudebede. Han ligner en mand. En anden mand. Er du klar over, at nr han fr erektion -. Jeg vil ikke hre det. Han forklarede det. Pikken bliver fuld af destilleret vand. Fra et reservoir i den hjre balde. Det gldede mig sdan set, men jeg var besluttet p at forblive helt kold.Hvad nu, hvis vi laver robotter, som er bedre end os, venligere end os og mske har bedre hukommelse end os og en mere konsistent evne til at bedmme verdens sprgsml? Mske ville vi elske dem og blive snderknust, hvis vi skulle leve uden dem. Tnker du, at det er jagten p det perfekte menneske? Ja, det er en jagt efter gud, men ogs forsget p at blive gud. Et forsg p at kompensere for vores mangler som mennesker, siger McEwan.I romanen ' Maskiner som mig' viser det sig, at robotten tnker mere stringent end mennesker. Uden at spolere for meget kan det fortlles, at Miranda et sted i fortiden har fet en mand i fngsel.Han blev dmt for en forbrydelse, han ikke havde beget. Til gengld var han get fri fra en anden forbrydelse, som han faktisk havde beget. Hvad gr den retfrdighedssgende i den situation? Skal det afslres, at Miranda har ljet, s hun risikerer fngsel, eller skal man bare tie stille, fordi forbryderen fik sin fortjente straf, om end for den forkerte forbrydelse? Og hvad mener robotten Adam om det? Hvordan skriver man lige algoritmen, som lrer en robot at stikke en lille, hvid lgn?, som Ian McEwan sprger.Alan Turing genoplivetDet var det matematiske geni Alan Turing, der i 1950 lagde navn til Turing-testen, som skulle afgre, om en maskine besad intelligens eller ej.Hvis man stillede en maskine en rkke sprgsml, som den derefter besvarede p en mde, som ikke var mulig at skelne fra et menneskes svar, havde den bestet testen.Turing var en af hovedkrfterne p det hold, der arbejdede p den britiske regerings kodecenter Bletchley Park under Anden Verdenskrig. Her brd de tyskernes hemmelige koder og reddede dermed mange liv p de allieredes side.Alan Turing begik selvmord i 1954. To r tidligere blev han dmt for homoseksuel aktivitet og plagt hormonbehandling.Samtidig mistede han sin sikkerhedsgodkendelse og dermed muligheden for at fortstte det arbejde, han var optaget af.Den bermte matematiker er nu skrevet ind i Ian McEwans roman om kunstig intelligens, selv om den foregr i 1982. Genoplivningen af Turing er dog langtfra den eneste kontrafaktuelle oplysning, som lserne prsenteres for.Ud over at opfinde de nrmest perfekte menneskerobotter prsenterer McEwan ogs andre tekniske fremskridt, som ikke var en del af tidsperioden. Samtidig lader han Margaret Thatcher tabe Falklandskrigen i netop 1982. Med andre ord en sci-fi-roman med mere end et enkelt tvist. Men hvorfor? Jeg nskede, at denne roman skulle vre lsrevet i tid og alligevel have forbindelse til nutiden. Jeg ville have det hele p en gang. I stedet for at opfinde en hjteknologisk fremtid var jeg mere interesseret i, hvor hurtigt vi vnner os til teknologierne. I jeblikket brer vi rundt p smartphones som en del af vores liv, vores eksistens.Om 20 r klarer vi os mske uden.Hver gang teknologien passerer et nyt hjrne, bliver den kedelig. Jeg fr tit folk til at le, nr jeg fortller om dengang, jeg var i New York for 12 r siden og s folk st i lange ker ud p gaden. Jeg troede, de ville ind i en klub. Nej, nej, ld svaret. De stod i k for at f den nye i-Phone 3. Som Ian McEwan ser det, eksisterer der flere paradokser, nr det glder den nye, avancerede teknologi, som er blevet en del af vores hverdag. Nr der forsvinder arbejdspladser i Storbritannien, fordi man indfrer eksempelvis robotteknologi, er det ikke den kunstige intelligens, man skyder p.Nej, i stedet giver folk migranterne skylden, selv om vi faktisk har en hj beskftigelse.Den slags er ikke rationelt. Man kunne ogs tage de automatiske styringssystemer hos de Boeing-fly, der styrtede ned. Men hvis vi nogensinde fr skabt en kunstig mand, hvordan vil vi s vre i stand til at opdage det? Inden for kunstig intelligens findes der en skole, som mener, at vi p et tidspunkt rder over maskiner, der kan skanne hen over den menneskelige hjerne og imitere alle hjernens neuroner.Ja, at vi mske endda kan dyrke dem. Tror du, det vil ske? Alts, det handler om cirka 100 billioner neuroner. Det vil vre en del at kopiere.Mske kan vi imitere sm dele af hjernen, men jeg ved det ikke, siger Ian McEwan.Med et smil gr han opmrksom p, at hver gang vi ser en eller anden maskine, vi kalder en robot, stikker der et stort, tykt kabel ud af ryggen p den, og s lnge vi ikke har lst batteriproblemet, er der nok langt til frembringelsen af noget, der har en slags bevidsthed.Men hvad med ham selv? Er han ogs en slags forfattermaskine, der konstant leder efter emner, han kan skrive om? Selv om folk har svrt ved at tro det, s er det ikke srlig kalkuleret. Det handler som regel om, at min hjerne bevger sig rundt i bestemte retninger.Nogle gange sprger folk, om jeg laver research til mine romaner, men sdan tnker jeg ikke p det. Det handler bare om, hvor min nysgerrighed frer mig hen. Nr han begynder at skrive, skitserer han heller ikke handlingen p forhnd, og de fleste af ideerne opstr, mens han skriver.Det er ligesom at tale sammen. Man planlgger ikke, hvad man vil sige, fr man siger det. Udviklingen af en roman er en udvidet og mere kompliceret version af det. Derfor har jeg altid tnkt p romaner som undersgelser, som rejser. Mens vi sidder og taler om blandt andet ' Solar', funderer McEwan over, at klodens klimaproblemer kun er blevet vrre, siden han skrev romanen i 2010. Emnet optager ham stadig, og da jeg sprger, om han vil skrive om klimaforandringerne igen, indeholder hans svar samtidig forklaringen p, hvordan han egentlig fr ideen til sine romaner.En roman skal vre bygget op af detaljer, ikke af tematikker, og jeg var ret heldig med ' Solar'. Jeg researchede ikke til en roman, men var bare interesseret i klimaforandringer. Derfor var jeg taget til en konference om sagen i Potsdam uden for Berlin. Jeg var inviteret til at holde tale for deltagerne, og der var 50-60 nobelpristagere til stede. De var stort set alle mnd i 60' erne og 70' erne, og det var der, jeg opdagede vejen ind til en roman. Ude i salen s jeg disse alfahanner fra videnskaben. De var ikke lngere aktive inden for forskningen. I stedet holdt de taler, udarbejdede forskningsansgninger og styrede store afdelinger.Der s jeg ideen: at min hovedperson skulle vre en mand, som levede i sin egen skygge. Han fik engang nobelprisen, men efter det var det slut med hans forskning. Jeg skal vre heldig at finde noget tilsvarende, hvis jeg skal kunne skrive om klimakrisen igen.Falske begyndelserEn af de tidligere gange jeg mdte Ian McEwan, fortalte han, at hans skrivebordsskuffer er fyldt med det, han kalder falske begyndelser. I dag taler han mere om, at disse forsg handler om at tve.Det handler meget kreativitet om.Man m teste en id over tid. Noget kan lyde godt om torsdagen, men er det mske ikke p lngere sigt. Hvis de samme ting bliver ved med at dukke op, er det et godt tegn. Lgger du s ideen til side? I stedet for at udvikle det til en roman skriver jeg rundt om det. Da jeg tnkte p ' Maskiner som mig', prvede jeg at skrive en novelle om kunstig intelligens.Den handler om en mand, der elsker med en robot 300 r fremme i tiden. Det er en tid, hvor det er virkelig uforskammet at sprge nogen, om de er gte mennesker. Alle er helt ens. Robotterne har fet rettigheder og pligter. De er blevet gode bankfolk og lger, og man aner ikke engang, om premierministeren er en robot. Dermed er vi helt fremme ved i dag, for Ian McEwans seneste lille udgivelse har netop premierministeren som en central figur. Han skrev bogen hen over sommeren. Den hedder ' The Cockroach' og er en Brexit-satire. Titlen hentyder til, at en kakerlak forvandles til landets premierminister.Vil du skrive mere om Brexit? Hvis det gr galt for os - og det kunne det meget nemt - er vi p vej til noget, som er uundgeligt. Mske er det ikke hovedemnet, mske er det bagtppet, men det vil vre en del af alle de romaner, der vil blive skrevet i Storbritannien.Det bliver et virkeligt vendepunkt i nationens skbne.' Kropsbevidsthed

Max Versace og Heather Ames havde hnderne fulde i januar.Det var to nyfdte, der krvede deres opmrksomhed: snnen Gabriel og den virtuelle rotte Animat.Som s mange andre spdbrn var Gabriel fra fdslen udstyret med en hjerne, der satte ham i stand til at foretage sig enkle ting s som at gribe fat i noget, sutte og se slrede billeder af sine forldre. Resten var op til ham selv.Fra den allerfrste dag hans krop stiftede bekendtskab med verden, begyndte hans sanser at reagere. Han lrte at flge en genstand, der bevgede sig, med jnene, at skelne mellem rdt og gult og at rkke ud efter sin mor. Som rene gr, vil han lre at kravle, g og tale for til sidst at kunne klare sig selv.Hvis det gr efter planen, vil Animats udvikling flge et lignende mnster. Den var heller ikke programmeret i udtalt grad ved "fdslen".Men i samspil med sin virtuelle verden har Animat allerede lrt at skelne mellem farver og f rede p omgivelserne. Efterhnden som den udvikler sig, vil den ved hjlp af sine sanser lre endnu mere.Intelligens afhngig af kroppenAnimats "forldre" er forskere ved Boston University.De forsger at bygge intelligente maskiner, dvs. kunstig intelligens, ud fra den mest intelligente maskine, vi kender til, nemlig hjernen.Men i stedet for at programmere selve hjernen skeler de til biologien.I lighed med menneskebrn og til forskel fra hovedparten af den kunstige intelligens, vi kender, vil udviklingen af Animats intelligens afhnge af, hvordan dens krop oplever verden.De to forskere hber, at kunstig intelligens p den mde kan n det punkt, hvor robotter begynder at tnke p en mere menneskelig mde.Det har vist sig at vre ikke s lidt af en udfordring.Tag f. eks. Watson, IBM's supercomputer, der i februar lb med sejren foran nsen p to menneskelige deltagere i quizzen Jeopardy p amerikansk tv. Det afsgte omfattede databaser, der var lagrede p 90 servere. Energiforbruget var 80 kilowatt - nok til at forsyne en lille by. I sammenligning hermed er vores hjerne njsom.En hjerne bruger mindre energi end en elpre og optager mindre plads end en toliters sodavandsflaske, siger datalogen Dharmendra Modha fra IBM.Men energi og rum er forhold af praktisk art.Et vsentligere problem er, at computerbaseret afsgning af en lang rkke lsningsmuligheder ikke kan mle sig med en rigtig hjernes funktion.Menneskelig intelligens udspringer ikke af logik, men af vores evne til at reagere p noget, der er uklart, og tilpasse os situationer, der ndrer sig hurtigt.Teorien om, at menneskelig erfaring kan sttes p logiske formler, viste sig at vre en meget forkert antagelse, siger Rolf Pfeifer, der forsker i kunstig intelligens ved Universitt Zrich.Naturen br efterlignes For mange r siden begyndte forskere at pldere for, at man skulle g anderledes frem. I 1980' erne kom Rodney Brooks fra Massachusetts Institute of Technology med et alternativ. Han hvdede, at det var bagvendt at begynde med at programmere komplekse egenskaber, nr vi ikke engang vidste, hvordan vi skulle skabe en elementr kunstig intelligens, der kunne undg at stde ind i en vg. Han mente, at vi i stedet burde efterligne naturen, der har givet os sanser, som gr os i stand til at overleve uafhngigt af hinanden i en umiddelbar verden. For at kunne bygge maskiner, der kan tnke uden at modtage direkte ordrer, skal vi frst bygge deres kroppe, ld Brooks' teori.Den var ikke helt ved siden af. I 1989 byggede han Genghis, en seksbenet insektrobot, der kunne bevge sig fremad, selv om den ikke havde et centralt styringssystem. Dens sensorer reagerede umiddelbart p feedback, som den modtog i samspillet med omgivelserne. Efterhnden som robotten f. eks. bevgede sig af sted, modtog den nye pvirkninger fra omgivelserne, og disse ndringer styrede de bevgelser, den derefter foretog, sledes at den blev i stand til at passere omrder, den ikke udtrykkeligt var blevet programmeret til at passere.Kropsliggjort bevidsthedForskningen i neurobiologi, kognitiv videnskab og filosofide nste 10 r gav et fingerpeg om, at Brooks' teori gjorde sig gldende i endnu bredere forstand.I slutningen af 1990' erne fremsatte George Lakoff, der forsker i kognitiv videnskab ved University of California, Berkeley, USA, en teori om, at ogs menneskelig intelligens er ulseligt forbundet med den mde, hvorp vore kroppe og sanser vekselvirker med omgivelserne.Iflge Lakoff og hans tilhngere forklarer vor "kropsliggjorte bevidsthed" ikke blot basal intelligens, som f. eks. hvordan vi lrte visuelt at genkende genstande, men ogs kompliceret, abstrakt tankegang. Her havde man endelig nglen til at skabe avanceret, menneskelignende intelligens.Der var blot t problem: Kropslig kunstig intelligens er vanskelig at opgradere. For at opgradere en robots krop, der er forsynet med sensorer, skal der ikke blot programmeres ekstra funktioner, men selve sensorerne skal ogs afmonteres og genmonteres, og det er mjsommeligt.Trods disse vanskeligheder fandt et par forskere tanken for interessant til ikke at forsge sig, og i 2009 skabte Owen Holland fra University of Sussex, Brighton, en menneskelignende robot, Eccerobot, der var bygget med udgangspunkt i nogle af de principper, der l bag Genghis.Eccerobot kan dog stadig ikke foretage selv simple bevgelser og har ikke udvist tegn p intelligens. S selv om forbedret computerkapacitet har virket befordrende p konventionel kunstig intelligens, gr det trgt med at udvikle kropslig kunstig intelligens.Forskerne sndFor et par r siden begyndte Versace og hans medarbejdere for alvor at tro p, at der trods alt var hb for kropsliggrelse af intelligens - hvis man skippede den fysiske krop. Ved hjlp af nye kraftige grafikkort kan videospildesignere simulere alting, inklusive en robots krop, dens omgivelser og endog de naturkrfter, som samspillet mellem krop og omgivelser er baseret p.Grafikkort bner nye muligheder, siger Ennio Mingolla, der har specialiseret sig i kognitiv videnskab og er en del af Animat-teamet.Han og resten af teamet greb chancen og "snd" med kropsliggrelsen. I stedet for at bakse med en virkelig krop byggede de en virtuel krop, hvis kunstige sensorer skulle virke i samspil med et omhyggeligt skabt virtuelt milj. P den mde, rsonnerede de, kunne de udnytte alle fordelene ved kropslig kunstig intelligens uden at dje med ulemperne. Gik det efter planen, ville de hastigt kunne komme videre med at udvikle en kropslig intelligens.Animat blev fdt den 11. januar. Det var dagen, da Versace og hans medarbejdere tilkoblede dens hjerne, der bestr af hundredvis af neurale modeller - hvortil er knyttede bl. a. farvesyn, motorik og angst - som alle er tro kopier af de tilsvarende biologiske mekanismer.Det betyder, at de ikke indeholder en liste over direkte kommandoer, prcis ligesom menneskesnnen Gabriels hjerne ikke beregner dimensionerne p hans vugge for at finde ud af, hvor han skal rkke ud efter et stykke legetj.Der er i bund og grund ingen regler. Alt er skabt s tt som muligt p det biologiske sidestykke, siger Versace.S nr Animat skal lre noget og bevge sig, er den i lighed med Genghis afhngig af den feedback, den fr fra sin virtuelle "krop", som er udstyret med den slags sensorer, der findes i huden og nethinden. Men til forskel fra Genghis kan hver del af Animat opgraderes p et jeblik.Dens omgivelser overholder ogs den virkelige verdens fysiske love, herunder tyngdeloven, og giver Animat realistiske sanseoplysninger. Lyset rammer f. eks. dens virtuelle nethinde og giver den farvesyn, og p dens simulerede hud udves korrekt justerede tryk - af f. eks. vand og luft. Forskellige kombinationer af sdanne pvirkninger ligger til grund for Animats reaktioner.Animats virtuelle verden er en gigantisk bl swimmingpool omgivet af stnger i forskellige farver. Som sine virkelige artsfller hader Animat vand. Det skyldes en angstfornemmelse, som Mingolla har indsat i dens neurale model. Animat kan kun slippe for vandet ved at finde en lille afsats, der er skjult under vandets overflade. Prven p Animats intelligens er, hvor hurtigt den lrer at finde denne redningsplanke.Panik i poolen Det frste forsg tegnede ikke lovende: Animat svmmede panisk rundt i forskellige mnstre i en time, fr forskerne gav op og indstillede forsget.Da de smed Animat i poolen for anden gang, ndrede dens svmmemnster sig imidlertid.Denne gang, efter 45 minutters svmning i et nyt mnster, fandt den afsatsen. Dens angstniveau blev mrkbart mindre, da den kom op af vandet, og den belnning, som det var udtryk for, styrkede samspillet mellem de faktorer, der frte den derhen. Animat kendte f. eks. nu farven p stngerne i nrheden af afsatsen, og den kendte ogs den omtrentlige bane, den skulle flge.Og ganske rigtigt - den tredje gang Animat blev smidt i vandet, svmmede den rundt i kortere tid, fr den fandt afsatsen, fordi den kikkede efter plene med de rigtige farver. Fjerde gang tvede den ikke, men satte kurs direkte mod afsatsen. Der fik vi beviset. Dyrets intelligens var ikke til at tage fejl af, siger Versace.I lbet af nogle r vil Versaces team skabe flere animater, der kan noget mere. De vil ogs visuelt kunne kende genstande samt hre og endog kommunikere med hinanden. Den optimistiske tidsramme ville ikke vre mulig uden den genvej, som de virtuelle kroppe er udtryk for.Hvor lovende disse frste forsg s end mtte vre, er den virtuelle verden imidlertid kun et velsesomrde. Frst nr Animats virtuelle krop skiftes ud med en rigtig krop, skal projektet virkelig st sin prve. Det ultimative ml er alt andet lige en robot, der kan bevge sig selvstndigt i den rigtige verden.Lettere at programmereFordelen ved en sdan robot frem for en traditionelt skabt kunstig intelligens er, at der ikke skal tages hjde for alle mulige situationer i programmeringen, som sledes vil blive mindre omfattende.Det betyder mindre hardware, og mindre hardware betyder mindre energiforbrug. Og her er vi ved sagens kerne: Udvikling af en robot, der kan bevge sig, trffe sine egne beslutninger og overleve i den virkelige verden uden at skulle bruge ret meget "brndstof" - ligesom os.Fremtidsudsigterne for en sdan maskinintelligens er baggrunden for en henvendelse fra den amerikanske rumfartsadministration, Nasa, tidligere p ret. En Mars-robot med biologisk intelligens kunne lre at bruge sin neurale model til at se, til at holde balancen og til at bevge sig vk fra ufremkommeligt terrn, s det ikke lngere var ndvendigt, at flge dens frd nje.En sdan robot ville ogs kunne planlgge sin rute, udforske omgivelserne, kontrollere sit batteriniveau og vende sikkert tilbage til sin base, siger Versace, hvis team allerede arbejder p en virtuel Mars til Animat med kratere og hele pibetjet.Er det dyremishandling?Eftersom Animat er udformet til at lre som sine biologiske artsfller, har nogle velkendte sprgsml meldt sig. Kan Animat fle smerte? Den udsttes immervk for negativ forstrkning i form af betydelig angst og positiv forstrkning i form af jeblikkelig lettelse, nr den nr frem til den skjulte afsats.I bund og grund begynder den jo at danne sig en forestilling om, hvad der er godt, og hvad der er drligt, siger Versace. S hvornr skal han begynde at tnke p dyremishandling? Sprgsmlet er mske ikke s dumt, som det lyder. Evnen til at fle eller fornemme noget kunne vre et vigtigt bindeled mellem intelligens og bevidsthed. Nogle forskere i kognitiv videnskab mener, at menneskelig bevidsthed er et produkt af grundlggende forstrkningsmekanismer som angst og lettelse. "Flelsen" af bevidsthed - den indre oplevelse af at se rdt eller fle smerte - udspringer ikke af en hjere erkendelse, men af et simpelt samspil med verden.Flelse er ikke noget, som vore hjerner udvirker, men noget, som vi foranlediger, en mde at vre i samspil med verden p, siger Kevin O'Regan fra institut for perceptionspsykologi p Universit Paris Descartes i Frankrig.Hverken Max Versace eller Heather Ames tror, at Animat vil udvikle bevidsthed. Men de er ogs ligeglade: De har nemlig nok at gre med den kropslige biologiske intelligens, som de har avlet p den gammeldaws facon.New Scientist 2011 New Scientist. Alle rettigheder reserveret.Distribueret af Tribune Media Service.Oversttelse: Anna Marie Andersen.newton@jp.dk ,, Teorien om, at menneskelig erfaring kan sttes p logiske formler, viste sig at vre en meget forkert antagelse.Rolf Pfeifer Forsker i kunstig intelligens ved Universitt Zrich.Fakta: ANIMATS RUTEMan tester Animats intelligens ved at mle, hvor hurtigt den kan finde afsatsen (bl skive) i vandet, som er en del af Animats virtuelle verden.Ved frste forsg fandt Animat aldrig afsatsen.
